\begin{itemize}

<score>S</score>

So if he can achieve a + sqrt(2 - a^2) > 3λ, then Alice loses at n=3. If not, then Alice can survive to n=3, and then she can choose x_3 = 3λ - S_2 (if S_2 ≤ 3λ). She can then set S_3 = 3λ, and Q_3 = Q_2 + (3λ - S_2)^2. Then the game continues. So we can analyze this as a "two-player" game where each player chooses numbers to maximize their chance to force a win.

This is getting complicated. Perhaps there is a known result or a clever transformation. Let's try to think of the game in terms of "infinite sequences" that satisfy the constraints. The game ends if at some turn, the cumulative sum (or square sum) exceeds the bound. So the player who can force the opponent to be in a position where the cumulative sum of squares is greater than the bound at the start of his turn loses. Since the bounds increase linearly, the "critical" condition for the game to continue indefinitely is that there exists a sequence x_n such that for all n, S_n ≤ λ n and Q_n ≤ n (for even n) and also Q_{n-1} ≤ n for odd n? Actually, as argued, for infinite play, we need Q_n ≤ n+1 for odd n, and S_n ≤ λ (n+1) for even n. That is a necessary condition. But we can also consider the "maximal" possible S and Q under optimal play.

Thus we need to analyze the game more systematically. The game is a kind of "alternating" game where each player chooses a number to maximize their chance to force a win. To determine the outcome, we can try to compute the "critical" λ where the game is a draw. This is similar to "two-player" games with "budget" constraints; sometimes the threshold is given by the solution of a fixed point equation.

Let's try to think of the game in terms of "strategy stealing" or "mirror" for the opponent. Perhaps we can consider the following: For Alice to have a winning strategy, she can choose x_1 = λ. Then the game proceeds as described. The condition for Bazza to survive is that at each even step, Q_{n-1} ≤ n. This imposes a condition on λ. If λ is large enough, the recurrence will cause Q_{odd} to exceed the bound eventually. We need to find the smallest λ such that Alice can force a win. That λ might be the positive root of some equation. Similarly, for Bazza to have a winning strategy, he might adopt a strategy that ensures that at some odd step, S_n > λ (n+1). That likely happens for λ small enough.

Let's attempt to analyze the recurrence for the "maximal" strategy for Alice. Suppose Alice always chooses the maximal allowed x_n to bring S_n to λ n. Then the state after her move is (S=λ n, Q). Then Bazza chooses x to minimize the resulting Q after his move (subject to his constraint). But we also need to consider that he might want to win directly, not just survive. However, if he cannot win immediately, he will try to survive as long as possible. So we can model the game as: at each even turn, Bazza chooses x to minimize the eventual Q (or to avoid giving Alice a win). That is, he will choose x that minimizes the expression that determines future Q. Since his goal is to avoid losing, he will choose x to keep Q as low as possible (or to keep S low? Actually, he wants to avoid that at her next turn, Q exceeds the bound. So he will choose x to minimize Q_{n+2}. That is what we did.

Thus the recurrence is: Starting with Q_1 = λ^2, then for each odd m, we have Q_{m+2} = (m+1) + (2λ - sqrt(m+1 - Q_m))^2 if sqrt(m+1 - Q_m) ≤ λ, else Q_{m+2} = (m+1) + (2λ - λ)^2 = (m+1)+λ^2 if sqrt(m+1 - Q_m) ≥ λ. But note that for λ>1, we have Q_1 = λ^2, and sqrt(2-λ^2) ≤ λ? For λ between 1 and √2, sqrt(2-λ^2) is between 1 and 0, and λ is between 1 and 1.414. So sqrt(2-λ^2) ≤ λ? For λ=1.2, sqrt(2-1.44)=0.7483 <1.2, so yes. For λ=1.4, sqrt(2-1.96)=sqrt(0.04)=0.2 <1.4, so yes. For λ=1.41, sqrt(2-1.9881)=sqrt(0.0119)=0.109 <1.41, so yes. So for λ>1, we have sqrt(2-λ^2) ≤ λ? The inequality sqrt(2-λ^2) ≤ λ is equivalent to 2-λ^2 ≤ λ^2 => 2 ≤ 2λ^2 => λ^2 ≥ 1 => λ ≥ 1. So for λ≥1, sqrt(2-λ^2) ≤ λ holds. For λ=1, equality. So in the regime λ≥1, we have sqrt(2-λ^2) ≤ λ, so Bazza's feasible x is at most sqrt(2-λ^2) which is ≤ λ. So the vertex x=λ is not feasible; he must choose x ≤ sqrt(2-λ^2) < λ. Thus the minimum f(x) is at the largest feasible x, i.e., x = sqrt(2-λ^2). So he will choose that. Then Q_2 = 2, and the recurrence for Q_3 is as before: Q_3 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2. So the recurrence is valid for all odd indices as long as the condition sqrt(n+1 - Q_n) ≤ λ holds. But we need to check if at some later odd n, sqrt(n+1 - Q_n) might become greater than λ.
